WHOSE FINGERS CLOSED IN A DEATH CLUTCH ON THROAT OF NORA FULLER
Ti.HE.work of establishing the identity t of the foul fiend who strangled little Nora Fuller to .death; in- the house; at: 221 1 Sutter street is still baffling the police. The strangler whose brutal
fingers cruelly shut off the life of his girlish victim masqueraded under so many names it will .be;almost.impossible to trace: him. Attorney Hugh Grant, at his own request, was shown
yesterday to the people who had dealings with the strangler while he was plotting his hellish -crime,;^nd they all agreed without hesitation that the attorney was not the man.

Fuller for purposes of identification. The

[ACH day that passes contributes ad
ditional mystery to the movements
of the fiend who strangled Nora
Fuller. All sorts of theories are be
ting advanced, but nearly every prob
able explanation of the motive and
the details of how the crime was com
mitted are shattered by some circum
stance which . readily disproves them.
The police are practically at sea. In
the history of crime in,this city the de
tectives have never had to deal with a
fmore j perplexing murder. The famous
Durrani case was a matter of easy solu
tion- when compared with the mystery
surrounding the strangling of Nora Ful
ler. In the Durrant case the Identity of
the murderer was known within a few
hours after the finding of the dead bodies
of his victims, and all that remained for
the police to do was to unearth the evi
dence that would fasten the crime upon
him.
The.inquest over the body of the mur 7
dered'girl was commenced yesterday. The
identity of John Bennett, alias C.B. :
Hawkins, alias Scott, is' still a mystery,
upon which yesterday's developments
threw.no light. .â¢ Â¦â¢

Several letters found at the Sutter
street house; it will be remembered, were
redirected . and the | supposition was that
the mail had been forwarded by the
postofflce, but investigation yesterday dis
closed the fact that the officials at the
general postoffice have no record of hav
ing been, requested . to forward letters to
"Mr. and Mrs. C. B. Hawkins" at 2211
Sutter street. Chief of^Polico Wittman
and Captain Seymour detailed Detective
T.P. Riordan on the case yesterday, and
Superintendent Cox gave the officer every
facility at the general postoffice. Realiz
ing the importance 1 of the investigation;
Mr. Cox gave Detective Riordan* a letter
to Superintendent A. Tyler of Station F
postofflce^ requesting that everything
should be ; done to aid the officer !n his
Inquiries. Riordan then visited Isadore
Coleman Levey, the postman who deliv
ered: the letters addressed "C. B. Haw
kins, Esq., 2211 Sutter street," and "Mrs.
C. B. Hawkins."
Levey was unable to throw any . light
on the mystery. He had neither seen nor
heard any one at the Sutter-street house.
Not getting a reply to his repeated ring
ing of the bell he pushed the. letters' he
had to deliver under the door.
At the Golden West Hotel, from -where
the letters were redirected, inquiries were
made, but all knowledge of them was
disclaimed. '

May Be Bennett
The police have discovered that on the
night of January 11 a man registered at
the Golden West Hotel under the name
of B. C. Bennett. He gave his place of
residence as Port Costa, but further than
that nothing of his identity is known by
the hotel people. . .
Attorney Grant was brought down to
police headquarters yesterday morning
and again questioned by Captain Sey
mour. Grant was asked if he ever knew
a man named Bennett, and he answered
that he did. He said that he once tran
sacted some legal business for a printer
named George L. Bennett, but it was
years ago. The . Bennett in question
proved to be a respectable citizen living
at 224 Broderlck street. He called volun
tarily at police headquarters last evening
and satisfied Chief Wittman and Captain
Seymour that he had no connection with
the case. ,
Traveled Under Aliases.
The detectives are satisfied that the
names the strangler gave to the different
people he spoke to in carrying out his
horrible plans are fictitious. It is quite
unlikely and unnatural that a man bent
on committing such a heinous crime as
he had in view would be so Indiscreet as
to give â¢ his right name. Although the
strangler dropped - into the Popular res

ler girl's association with Hugh Grant

LOS ANGELES, Feb. U.â Dramatic in
the extreme was the climax of the battle
between the Huntlngton-Hellman and the
Clark-Hook street railway interests to
day. Not In recent years has such, a con
test for supremacy between two rival
railroads been fought. While the mem
bers of the City Council listened unmoved
to the appeals of some of the foremost
attorneys of the city a crowd of specta
tors breathlessly heard the speeches that
brought to a close a contest that revealed
Senator Clark as a formidable rival of
the Huntington-Hellman syndicate for
supremacy in Los Angeles railway circles.
The fight arose over the granting of
franchises for street railways, for which
bids were opened yesterday. B. Murray,
the mysterious person who bid $415,000 for
the Sixth and Seventh Ward franchise,
and was given twenty-four hours in which
to deposit the money, failed to "malte
good." This leffE. A. Davis' bid of
$133,000 the next highest, and only, to-day
it was learned that Davis represented the
Clark interest.

Judge Gear, the presiding Judge ~of the
term, ordered the Immediate trial of pris
oners formerly convicted by methods he
held some months ago to have been ille
gal, arid since Indicted for the same of
fenses by a Grand Jury. The Attorney
General Is prosecuting the men under the
new indictments and at the same time is
holding that the old convictions are valid.
This question wil come up in the United
States Supreme Court next April. '
Gear originally released the prisoners
because they had been tried without
Grand Jury - indictments and convicted
without unanimous jury verdicts after
the annexation. Attorney General Dole,
holding that United Statfes laws regard
ing such matters did not extend to Ha
waii by virtue of the resolution by which
the islands were annexed, nor. until the
Hawaiian Territorial act took effect, took
an appeal; though Gear, denied the prose
cution's right of appeal in such cases.
The Supreme Court of the Territory sus
tained the Attorney General, but "United
States District Judge Estee rendered a
decision on habeas corpus according with
thatof Gear.' -
Since Kstee's- decision Judge Gear has
held to his view as therein , indorsed in
spite of the Territorial ; Supreme Court's
decision the other way. He declines to
recognize the old trials as valid. _ The At
torney General's department presented
the cases over again to a Grand Jury and
had new indictments brought, and the
cases came .up for trial again -this term.
Attorney General Dole had appealed one
of tho cases, that of Osaki Mankichi,
charged with murder, to the United States
Supreme Court, and he asked that all the
cases bo postponed untir that one is de
cided. The attorneys -for the defendants
demanded immediate i trials under the in
dictments, and Gear refused the continu
ances "asked for by the prosecution. Dole
asked the TerltorlaT Supreme Court foV
an injunction to stop Gear from hearing
the first case, which had been begun, but
.the Supreme Justices refused to issue an
Injunction. . _ . .-; â¢

it et endeavor to look pleased, for I'm your King,

Else these proceedings can't proceed.
Leave noise and to sweet silence cling
For Just a minute, and give the King
A chance to nay his little say
And lack of wisdom to display
To your admiring ears.

MERRY THRONG IN THE MAZES
OF A WALTZ AT THE ANNUAL
MARDI GRAS FESTIVAL.
The boxes at each end of the gallery
were in carnival colors and were an at
tractive feature of tbe decorations.
The Prince's throne on the north side
of the gallery was richly decorated with
Oriental draperies.
The Seaxles gailery was decorated with
thousands of yujrds of garlands, crossing
a.nd recrossing under the skylight and
from which depended baskets of ferns,
vines and flowers, festooned with red rib
bons. The walls were covered with white
drapery and over this was festooned myr
tle and intermingled with the myrtle were
vari-colored electric lights. Where the
garlands were festooned to the wall bows
of red and yellow ribbons were affixed
completing the effect.
The maple room, which is situated be
tween the house gallery and the main gal
lery, was particularly dainty, the walls
being draped with a canary colored ma
terial, over which were spread ferns, in
termingled with poppies, and interspersed
among these were Incandescent lights,
which carried out the scheme of gold and
yellow. In the doorways hung magnificent
baskets of ferns. The display was largely
floral. Magnificent begonias on a cabinet
toned Into the general effect. At one end
of the room was a grove in which was a
statue of the Venus de Milo.
The interior of the Art Institute was
never more beautifully nor more elabo
rately decorated. Flowers and potted
plants were used with artistic effect. The
principal decorations in the house gal
lery, which was transformed for the oc
casion into the "court of the jesters,"
was a hanging illuminated chandelier,
composed of innumerable lanterns of car
nival yellow. An Immense lantern was
surrounded by several tiers of smaller
cues cf the same color lighted by Incan
descent lamps and festoons of lanterns
were strung to the four corners of the gal
leries. At each end were colored devices
with carnival masks in the center on
Japanese ' umbrellas. Redwoods and
bamboos, twenty or thirty teet high and
wreathes ot evergreens tied with parti
colored ribbons, added to the scene. There
was a notable absence of the draperies
which in past years tended to obscure the
architecture of this magnificent gallery.
On this occasion the decorations were
simple, but more beautiful than ever be
fcre.
Interior of tbe Art Institute is
Made Resplendent With
Color.

More than six hundred people attended
the carnival. Society was represented by
its leaders. Mrs. Herman Oelrichs, who
lately arrived from New York, received
her friends in a box. Edwin M. Green
way was in his element, superintending
things, and nearly every member of the
smart set was there.
In the gay throng were men distin
guished in the business world and many
who have earned fame in their profes
fcions. Army and navy sent large dele
gations and the officers were resplendent
in their uniforms. They appeared In
striking relief to the men in evening
cress who helped fill the place.
The ladies had the privilege of wearing
masks until the midnight hour and had
great fun saying: soft nothings to men
iney knew intimately. There was a be
v/itching array of costumes, moving in a
bewildering mass of color and impersona
tion throughout the rooms, every lady be
ing beautifully garbed.
Kingr Carnival and his queen, Mr. and
Mrs. G. Cadenasso, set a brilliant and
dazzling pace for their followers in the
matter of raiment The carnival queen
wore a regal robe in Marie Stuart style
of white satin and red silk/ elaborately
embroidered with spangles. Her court
robe of rich red velvet was edged with
ermine. Jeweled crown and necklace
completed the toilet. King Carnival was
a. dashing chevalier of the fifteenth cent
ury in his historic costume of red satin
and velvet with gorgeous jewels and trap
pings.

served for the assembled masqueraders.
ry. Gayly dressed cavaliers

CHICAGO, Feb. ll.-During a fight with
rifles this evening between the followers
of rival claimants to property lying along
the Lake Shore drive, the most aristo
cratic boulevard in Chicago. Frank Kirk,
a watchman for one of the claimants,
Â¦was ehot through the head and killed.
The property ...in dispute consists of
made land lying east of the Lake Shore
drive and between it and Lake Michigan.
Captain George W. Streetor, who has for
many years been a thorn in the side of
rvorthside property owners because of his
propensity to settle on vacant ground
and then claim in the courts the rights
of a squatter, has erected several small
phanties upon this ground and asserts
that. Inasmuch as it was not originally
Included in the Government survey of the
State of Illinois, it does not belong to
the State, but is public property open to
settlement.
To-night Henry L. Cooper, a lawyer,
who has been active in his opposition to
Watchman Is Killed Dur
ing Fierce Battle in
Chicago.

The question whether Powel Fredrick
or Joseph Windrow Is the legally consti
tuted clerk of the Justices' Court was
raised yesterday by Attorneys Eugene W.
Levy and Andrew G. Maguire, represent
ing defendants In a suit filed with Clerk
Fredrick.
W. J. Thompson and John N. Ross, de
fendants in a suit brought against them
by the National Law and Collection Com
pany, filed a demurrer and a notice of
motion to quash the summons and dis
miss the action with Clerk Windrow yes
terday. The ground of the demurrer is
that no summons has been properly is
sued by Clerk Powel Fredrick of the Jus
tices* Court or legally issued at all.' and
upon the further ground that no com
plaint has been legally filed in said ac-
Uon with the clerk of the Justices' Court.
The papers In the suit were filed with
Fredrick January 3L
The motion will be based upon the pa
pers and records in the case and -will be
heard before Justice of the Peace Dunn
to-morrow morning at 10 o'clock.
Since Clerk Windrow opened his office
on January 31 there have been iiled 170
complaints, while Clerk Fredrick has re
corded only twenty-one for the same
period. Of the twenty-one filed with
Fredrick fourteen are duplicates of suits
filed with Windrow.

A meeting of the general committee
having in charge the banquet to.be ten
dered Judge Jarmes F. Smith was held
last evening at the law offices of Sullivan
& Sullivan. The banquet will be given in
the Maple room of the Palace Hotel on
Saturday evening of this week, beginning
at 7:30' o'clock. "
The committee that had arranged for
a banquet at the time Judge Smith ar
rived; home, but which was postponed on
account, of the death of President Me-
Kinley and again because of the death of
a brother of the Judge, was in attendance
at the meeting last evening and assisted
in the deliberations of the present com
mittee.
A number of letters were received from
prominent citizens, including army and
navy officers and National Guardsmen,
which, taken into consideration with the
enthusiastic meeting of the committee, in
sures a pronounced success for. the ban->
quet, which is intended, to be a fitting
tribute to a son of California who has
achieved much distinction in his career
in the Philippines. >

Cabanlss yesterday afternoon. The com
plaining witness was A. Newman, man
ager of his wife's millinery store at 130
Sixth street, who alleged that he bought
from Jens for $500 one thousand shares of
a company having mines In Calaveras
County on the representation : that they
were very valuable. The Judge decided
that the alleged misrepresentations were
opinions rather than assertions of fact.
Streetor, accompanied by Policeman
O'Malley. went on the ground of the
"district" and was attacked by Streetor,
who knocked him down with the butt of
a revolver. Several of Streetor's follow
ers covered O'Malley with their weapons
and he was ordered to leave or be shot.
Shortly after Cooper and O'Malley had
left the ground a pitched- battle began
between three of Streetor's followers,
William McManners, William Blokie and
John Hoeldtke, and two watchmen em
ployed by Cooper, Frank Kirk and Sam
uel Porterous. Kirk and Porterous were
standing outside a small shanty of their
own, and one of the first shots fired
struck Kirk on the top of the head. Por
terous entered the shanty and returned
the fire of the Streetor men with a rifle.
A riot call was hastily sent to the East
Chicago-avenue police station and a
wagon filled with policemen was sent on
the run toward the place. When the
officers arrived Kirk's body lay In the
snow outside his shanty, while from the
window Porterous kept up a steady fire
upon the three men in the house, who
were returning his fire with all the speed
with which they could work their rifles.
The police arrested Streetor and all the
participants in the fight.

As an illustration -of the heaviness of the' work
often imposed;by.requests for information, it is relat^
ed that a Senator, recently introduced .a resolution

sation. ; ' '-,,; .Â¦Â¦'.â¢â¢ : : c - -
Owing to public interest in all affairs of our insular
possessions and the prevailing ignorance concerning
them, the demand for information of one kind. or an
other concerning them has been so frequent as to im
posed heavy burden upon the Division of Insular
Affairs, and accordingly the officials have made a
protest. The public has thus been made" aware of tHe
extent to whichA'the abuse has been carried. â¢
Back of everything in this ; wbrld, however, there
is & question of cost. To furnish information : for
Congressmen requires, a considerable expenditure on
the part of the administration; and there are times
when the cost seems to be more than the information
is worth. It. takes only a few minutes of time for
either branch of Congress to ask the
but it takes a great deal of time for the clerks of the
bureau having the matter in charge to compile it, ar
range it and get it printed. '" J '
WHEN a member of Congress rises in his
seat and asks that some branch of the ad
ministration be requested to compile, print
and furnish Congress with information concerning
any part of the public business his motion receives a
prompt second and is nearly. always approved by a
majority of his fellow* members. In fact a request
for information is one of the hardest things in the
world to refuse. When a man. wishes to learn, some
thing in order that he may moreproperly. fulfill his
duties it appears nothing -more than just and right
that the information should.be given him.

Neither McCabe nor his railroad is, to be punished
for the .offense thus' frankly admitted. The "law .has
been violated, but it cannot be enforced; the commis
sion has been mocked, :.but it cannot vindicate itself.
Such a condition of affairs is not pleasing to a peo
ple who like our own have been brought up to have
a high respect for law and to look upon it as a safe
guard not only of justice. but of liberty. It is full
time for Congress to, take action in this matter. If
the law be unjust it should be repealed; if the com
mission be unworthy of respect it shotild'be abol
ished; but so long as the; one remains on the statute
book and the other represents the Government of
the United States the railroads should I be compelled

pany."

Â«r "You -mean to say, then, sir, that those records
were destroyed for the purpose of "removing evidence

"Why/not?" insisted the Commissioner.
At a recent hearing in Chicago several traffic man
agers were under examination by the commission
concerning the granting of rebate^. They had no
hesitation in admitting that rebates were granted
wherever it was deemed for the interest of the roads
to grant them. One of these officials, D. T. McCabe,
traffic manager of the Pennsylvania lines west of
Pittsburg, testified to giving secret reductions in
freight rates, and when asked for the records of the
office showing the transactions said they could not
be produced as the road did not have them. ' The
following conversation is then reported to have en
sued between McCabe and Commissioner Clements:
"No records of transactions- involving so much
money?" doubtfully asked the Commissioner.
"I do not think we kept them after they had served
their purpose."
If the. reports that come to us from the East be
accurate the commission had good grounds for mak
ing that complaint. It was not uttered upon a moral
conviction of the guilt of railway officials, but upon
a certain knowledge of it. In fact, so powerless is
the commission to enforce the law that it cannot en
force respect for itself, and railway men have no hesi
tation in frankly admitting violations of the law and
defying the commission to punish them. '
WHEN making their recent report to Con
gress the Interstate Commerce Commis
sion deplored: "That the leading traffic
officials of many of the principal railway lines, men
occupying high positions and charged with the most
important duties," should deliberately violate the stat
ute law of the land, and in some cases agree with
each other to do so; that it should be thought by
them necessary to destroy vouchers and so manipu
late bookkeeping las to obliterate evidence of the
transactions."

THE trial, of George'D.'f Glllespie, a
well known local .politician * and
lodging-house keeper ' r aÂ®U8 .Ninth
street, for the killing ;of William
F. Griffin, an apprentice^ plmnber,
on the evening of August 2O'iast,'.was be
gun In Judge Cook's court yesterday. ;
it is to be borne m mind, moreover, that many of
these fences erected in front' of vacant lots are raised
to. such a height and rented to advertisers at : such
prices as enable the owners to make a profit while
leaving the lot unused and vacant. Thus they tend to
check the upbuilding of"^the city and detract from
the value of the adjoining lo'tsi An injury is there
fore done not only tc the community at large but
to particular persons. Some remedy should be pro
vided by law for the mitigation -,of these .evils,' and if
nothing better can be devised we recommend .the
Supervisors to try taxation. 1'
There is no reason why they should not be taxed.
A fence is property and advertising is a trade. More
over bill board and fence advertising is like peddling,
inasmuch as it makes use of public thoroughfares,
â¢and therefore is rightty subject to municipal super
vision and control. Finally, such advertising is not
infrequently a gross nuisance, marring the appear
ance of the city, offending the eye and leading up to
the erection of huge* fences that are a serious danger
to passers on the streets and to "adjacent property by
reason of their liability to fire.
It is clear that where low fences or light railings
are erected along the top of an embankment more
than ten feet above the adjacent sidewalk no harm
h done. Such fences are not so unsightly as to de
face the city, neither are they, sufficiently heavy to
constitute a menace to persons on the street in time
of high winds, nor so inflammable as to be danger
ous when fires break cut. The unsightly and danger
ous fences are, in nearly every case, those which are
erected along business thoroughfares and used for
advertising. If then a tax be imposed upon such ad
vertising, or a license be required for the erection or
use of fences for advertising purposes, the fences
would then pass under the license laws of the city
and could be regulated'by taxation exactly as are
other licensed privileges. " - â <
While the decision seems technical, it rests upon
a fact which is beyond gainsaying. The configura
tion of the city is such that in 'many districts the
ground upon which houses are built and fences are
erected is more than ten feet above the adjacent side
walk. Hew to adapt tin ordinance to the irregularity
of the topography of the city will be found a perplex
ing problem, and perhaps it may have to be aban
doned. . %
Should that prove to be the case there remains
another method of dealing with the nuisance. The
Call has repeatedly directed attention to the good
results -obtained by the French Government in im
posing a system of taxation upon bill board adver
tising, and there is every reason to believe'that were
a similar system adopted in San Francisco a long
step would be taken toward eliminating the worst
evils of the high fence nuisance.
{In deciding the issue the Judge said: "In many
cases the surface of the ground upon which fences are
or may be erected in this city and county is more
than ten feet above the level of the adjacent sidewalk.
Upon such a lot no wooden fence whatever could be
erected without violating the ordinance. A fence one
inch high would be more than ten feet above the level
of the sidewalk. Inasmuch, therefore, as the ordi
nance denies to some property owners what is per
mitted to others it is not equal and uniform in its
operation and is, in my opinion, invalid."
JUDGE SEAWELL'S decision that the high
fence ordinance recently adopted by the Board
of Supervisors is invalid brings before that body
once more the problem of protecting the city against
the nuisance and the danger of high fences along
crowded thoroughfares. Since the present ordinance
is invalid a new one must b.e devised. The continu
ance of such fences is an indisputable evil and it is
incumbent upon the Supervisors to provide a remedy.

That lesson from Texas shouldnot be lost upon
California. It is to the. advantage, of all that, the pro
posed descriptive catalogue" be speedilyimd accurately
compiled, and cordial, assistance!? should be given
by all producers of every kind to the association in
completing, publishing and circulating -the work.
"Thirty-eight delegates from the New Ybrk.Mer
chants' Association and Chamber of Commerce, spe
cially appointed for the purpose, visited : Texas in
April last with the ultimate purpose of promoting the
investment of New York capital in industrial "devel
opment in Texas and of increasing' the trade of New
York with that State. Eleven sub-committees were
appointed, each charged with a special .' branch of
study. The party traveled- more than 6ooo miles and
visited thirty-seven important points in Texas. The
preliminary statement' of. the report of the, delega
tion, and one upon which' stress is laid, is that it was
assumed that ample dataas to the natural resources
of^ the State and their present development would be
readily accessible from official sources. Much to the
disappointment and embarrassment of the various
'committees, it was found that such data was exceed
ingly meager and did not furnish. a comprehensive
view of the natural resources and industrial condi
tions of the State. The report states, 'There is a no
table lack of such information as to .Texas, and in
all probability the State has been much prejudiced
thereby.'"
As an illustration of the need of compiling full in
formation concerning the State and its products the
prospectus says: â¢ . Â¦
While there have been a large number and variety
of publications on the subject of California products
there has been none that is thoroughly comprehen
sive in its nature. From each of them there has been
omitted something of importance to the* State and to
its commerce. The Â¦ consequence is that even well
informed Californians themselves" have but an inade
quate knowledge of. the industries carried" on
around them; and those who are most willing to pa
tronize home industry frequently make use of foreign
articles simply because they do not know of the ex
istence of home products of the same kind.
The object of the* catalogue, as stated by, the pros
pectus, is to make known the products of California
to' consumers both -at home and 1 abroad: It will be
given a free' general distribution throughout the
Union and jvill also be circulated in other countries.
It is not the intention to insert any paid advertise
ments and no charge .will; be made for a description j
of any Calif ornian' product.- On the "contrary, the,
prospectus announces that "full information regard
ing all products is solicited in order that all may. be
properly represented. It is desired to obtain the data
for each subject from those having special' knowledge
of it." : . / -

CALIFORNIA PRODUCTS.
Investigators in the" Capitol at ' Washington have"
discovered that; the. historic structure is infested with
millions of disease^germs, among them being that of
lockjaw. This particular microbe has not, however,'
affected any 'of the national orators in recent debate.
Genghiz Khan laid his empire like. a vast blanket
upon the world, from the Caspian to the Yellow Sea.
His descendant, Tamerlane, dominated from 'the
Euphrates to -the YangtserKiang, and set up the
peacock throne of the Moguls at Delhi. But all these
feats of arms were as nothing to what this old woman
has done by a decree that has no sword behind it but
her resistless will and awakened spirit. She was born
a slave, but she has liberated one-third of/ the human
race.
She goes to the front of the class composed of such
women as Semiramis, Tomyris, Zenobia and Kath
arine. ' The people of the Western nations should
meet her halfway in this determination to maintain
the unity and promote the progress of her vast em
pire. It is neither by territorial division, nor by in
vasion for selfish exploitation, that China is to.be
come a useful and contributing member of the family
of nations, but by an awakening. She has all the lore
and wisdom of the past. What Babylon and Egypt
had, she has. The virtues and ideals of Oriental phi
losophy are hers. Her people have 'morality, fidelity,
thrift. They possess the domestic virtues, a keen mer
cantile instinct and patience at toil surpassing that
of the ox. At present they feel only the wants of the
remote past. Their needs are primitive. As they go
up the path opened to them by the Empress Dowager
new needs will appear, with new duties and new
ideals. At the same time their capacity to trans
mute their resources to supply the needs of the West
ern world will increase, mutual exchange will grow
and out of normal commercial intercourse they and
the West will prosper. â¢ \ .
The old Empress" has gone further. She has de
creed the emancipation of her sex. By a. decree she
has forbidden foot binding, and from- her genius a
flash of light has illuminated farthest Cathay."
She has received the foreign; legations in the.For
bidden City and the sacred palace. .She has admitted
the wives of the foreign Ministers, replied to an ad
dress by Mrs. Conger, wife of our Minister, made s
her presents and sat with the ladies at tea. She has
broken the exclusiveness of the .Manchus. Hereto
fore the Manchus never went abroad. Chinese'were'
forbidden to intermarry- with them. They were the
stern and narrow rulers of the empire." Ignorant of
the world, self-poised, self-sufficient. The Empress
Dowager has broken their power. They are com
manded toÂ«educate their sons abroad, to permit inter
marriage with .the Chinese, and are practically put on
a level with the rest of the empire in its government.
For years the Chinese proper have known the rest of
the world, its laws, its civic institutions and its people. Â¦;
They have furnished, to China its great and learned^
men, Li Hung (Chang, Marquis Tseng, . Wu Ting
Fang, Ho Yow and others. Their ideas of regenera
tion and progress have been restrained by the narrow
Manchus. / .
Chinese royal -exclusiyeness and- austerity have
been forever banished. The kowtow is no more.

STOCKTON, Feb. ll.-Dr. G. A. Shurt
leff, who was superintendent of the Stock
ton Insane Asylum from 1865 to 1883, died
at 5 o'clock this morning in the Yosemito
Hotel, where he - had been | ill for several
years. His malady, was asphasla, a dis
ease of the mind. Dr. Shurtleff was a
member of Stockton's -first Council; was
elected County Recorder in 1855, and in
1S56 and again in 1SS3 was chosen a director
in the State ' asylum. He was an active
member of various medical societies, and
wrote many widely read articles regard
ing the treatment of Insanity. A brother
was formerly superintendent of the Ag
news asylum.

agement which was disposed to quibble

give wav to more liberal ideas, and also
that an open record of all transactions

to the insured.

Clouds of sand picked up by the gale
were - carried across the country with
fearful force, and at Wadsworth, Deseret,
Thistle, and other towns business was
practically suspended all afternoon, it be
ing almost impossible for one to breathe
while facing the drifting, whirling walls
of dust. At Deseret and Mirage the sand
rilled the air to such an extent that the
suti was obscured and lamps were neces
sary.
At Humboldt House, about forty miles
west of Winnemucca, the few persons
wno ventured out of doors beheld a pecu
liar sight. About eight miles south of
the town is a mountain rising abruptly
from the sandy desert The wind, sweep
ing across the flat country, caught the
scow of this hill and seemed to stretch it
out for two or three miles from the slopes.
Through occasional breaks in the clouds
of dust across the plain between the town
and the mountain the middle slopes could
be seen, apparently swept bare of snow in
a few hours by the terrific force of the
wind. The storm to-night shows but little
sign of abating.
FORT TOWNSEND, Feb. 11.â Captain
Kalstrom of the steamer Alice Gertrude,
arriving from Neah Bay this morning,
reports that the most furious storm of
the season prevailed down the straits and
off Cape Flattery last night. The wind
was from the southeast- and of hurricane
force. Towboats with vessels in tow were
untble to make headway and were com
pelied to seek shelter, while many sailing
vessels off Flattery, awaiting a chance to
get in, were driven far out to sea. No
casualties were reported, but shipping
men fear that â¢ some of the vessels will
meet mishap, as most of them are in
ballast. Â¦ .

BOY BATTLES FOB LIFE
WITH MADDENED BULL
Tularo County Youth Receives
Wounds That Hay Cause
His Death.
VISALIA, Feb. H.-Charles Howell. a
20-year-old youth living near this city,
had a terrible battle with a bull last night.
The animal attacked him unawares, strik
ing him in the back, knocking him down
and repeatedly; charging upon him. Finally
Howell grabbed the bull by the horns.
The enraged animal raised his head Into
the air with such force as to throw How
ell over the fence Into an adjoining field.
The boy was frightfully wounded and may
not recover. <.,_Â¦",
Rattler Bites Author James.
PHOENIX, Ariz., Feb. 1Lâ Professor
George "Wharton James, author and ' lec
turer Â«f New York City, was bitten on
the left thumb by a rattlesnake this af
ternoon. He was given medical attention
within four minutes and to-night is pro
nounced out of danger. The snake was a
big one in a cage and Jaxnes seized It by
the neck to lay it on the ground, so that
he could photograph it. The snake was
too strong for him and loosened Its head
enough to twist around and bite him.
The Bertha was built at Benicia by Mat
thew Turner in 1888. She was 185.5 feet
long, 32.5 of beam and 22.1 in length. She
had a registered tonnage of 926 tons.
During the first night on which the pas
sengers camped on the rock they suffered
no serious inconvenience, but on the sec
ond day a heavy rain set In, with a cold
wind that caused considerable suffering.
On Monday morning they were picked up
by. the Cottage City and brought to this
place. The loss of freight and baggago
by exposure to the elements Was a severe
blow to many of the passengers,' as they
had their last dollar invested in outfits for
the season's prospecting in Alaska,
On the approach of daylight the work of
lightering the vessel was commenced by
landing the horses and other freight. A
kedge was got out and the Bertha was
hauled into water again with the aid of a
donkey engine. As soon as she struck
deep water she began to sink, so she was
swung on to the beach, having lost her
keel, and having a large hole in her bot
tom, forward of the engine room. It is be
lieved that she will be a total loss.
All of the passengers were in bed. The
shock caused great confusion and a stam
pede was made for. the upper deck. Cap
tain Johansen and his officers quickly re
stored order, and on investigation it was
found that the vessel was In such
proximity to the shore that a" gangplank
would reach an elevated rock. The plank
was put into place and the passengers
walked ashore. Later bedding and bag
gage were landed and a camp was made
on the barren rocks.
The Bertha sailed from Seattle for Val
des. She encountered good weather until
Saturday when a storm set in and
on Sunday morning at 12:30 o'clock the
vessel struck on Harold Island.

VICTORIA, B. C, Feb.'lLâ At a meet-
Ing of the shareholders of the Pacific Ex
ploration and Development Company,
Â¦which has sent the treasure hunting ex
pedition to Cocos Island, Chairman H. H.
Jones to-day told of an offer that had
been made by Rear Admiral Palllser, for
merly In command on this station, to send
a man to guide the expedition to the hid
ing place of the pirates* loot In return for
a quarter of the buried millions for him
self and a twentieth for the man sent as
guide.
Rear Admiral Palliser had been with
one of the warships that went to Cocos
Island to search for the treasure. Accord-
Ing to his letter the sailors dug until thty
came to the planks and slabs which cov
ered the hidden gold. Then a half-ton of
dynamite was taken ashore and the whole
face of the rock blown out, Â¦ hundreds of
tons of rock being thus hurled down to
cover the hiding place of the treasure.
"This was done," explained Chairman
Jones, "because warships are not sup
posed to go on such a mission, and there
would have* been trouble had the Govern
ment known that they had, done so. More
over, as a British warship had turned
pirate and buried the treasure there, if a
warship had taken that treasure from the
island it is easy to Imagine what might
have happened. The sailors had it no
doubt in mind to rebury the treasure
when they found it and as soon as their
time expired with the British Government
they would go out and obtain the loot."

Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
111S Broadway. Feb. 11.
Local traffic on the broad-gauge line
was blocked for four hours, from 5 till
S o'clock to-night, by a wreck near the
Thirteenth-avenue station. The wrecked
train consisted of thirty empty flatcars
returning: from the mole, wnere a load
of dirt had just been dumped. Ten of tne
cars left the track and piled up on the
main line, whence It was necessary to
remove them before passenger trains
could be cot through.
The accident occurred as the engineer
of the freight train put on brakes as he
nearcd the switch. The .impetus of the
rear cars caused those nearest the engine
to "buckle," piling one on top of another
till a third of the train was ditched. The
engine did .not leave the rails.
While the wrecking crew were removing
the debris the 5 o'clock local. from Frult
valc, which was caught on the farther
Fide of the wreck, made trips from Thir
teenth avenue to the end of the line,
the passengers being transferred around
the wreckage from the Incoming locals.
No one was Injured in the accident.
Empty Flatcars Piled on
Main Track Stop Pas
senger Trains.

CREDITORS OF JACOBS
ARE ANXIOUS ABOUT HIM
BERKELEY,' Feb. 1Lâ Applications for
street railroad franchises, were received
last night by the Town Trustees from the
Oakland Transit Company. According to
law the franchises will be advertised and
sold to the highest bidder. The bids will
be opened on March 24 at a special meet
ing held for that purpose. The fran
chises asked for are for a line along Ade
line street and Shattuck avenue from the
southerly boundary line of this city to
Berkeley station; from Hearst avenue to
Euclid avenue, to Hiigard avenue; down
University avenue from Shattuck avenue
to Third street, and north along San
Pablo avenue from University avenue to
the town line.
The Transit Company has agreed to be
gin work as soon as granted the fran
chises and to complete the roads within
eighteen months. Work on the road to
West Berkeley will begin first.
Franchise Applications Hecsived.
OAKLAND, Feb. 11.â Gas escaping from
a gas stove nearly robbed Samuel Short,
an extraman In the Fire Department, and
his wife of their lives last night in their
apartments at the corner of San Pablo
avenue and Sixteenth street. Mrs. Short
was using the stove just before she and
her husband retired last night and In
some way one of the cocks became turned
around so that the gas leaked out slowly.
The facts that the gas only escaped in a
Email quantity and that the windows
were open were ail that saved the couple
from losing their lives. When an attend
ant passed their room this morning he
detected the odor of gas and broke open
the. door. He found the couple lying in
a semi-conscious staie. A physician was
called, who restored them to conscious
ness after some difficulty.

Deadly Fumes.

A. J. Morrison, the former cashier of the
Palace Hotel, his wife, who is a sister of
Mxs. Arthur Rogers, formerly Mrs. Alex
ander Montgomery, and his mother-in
law, Mrs. Green, a claimant of various
large properties, were parties to a lively
scrimmage in' Justice Quinn's court this
morning, in the course of which the
women were accused of theft and the
man of railroading his I wife into the in
sane asylum.
The immediate occasion of the quarrel
was a search warrant, sworn out by Mor
rison, for a strong box which he said his
wife and mother-in-law had taken from
his room at 663 Twenty-fourth street.
According to Mrs. Green the box con
tains papers bearing on Morrison's course
in the irregular incarceration of her
daughter in a madhouse. She does not
deny abstracting it from, his room, but
claims that she and her 'daughter had a
right there and were justified in takinr
the papers, which she considers of im
portance in securing her daughter's per
manent release.
When the party came into court ex
citement ran so high that Justice Quinn
was forced to continue the matter until
to-morrow and order all parties to be
represented by attorneys. Morrison at
tempted, to break into the box, but was
warned to desist by Quinn.
-The case is set for 11 o'clock to-morrow

A verdict was returned this evening; by
a Coroner's jury that Sieben came to his
death from a gunshot wound inflicted by
himself with suicidal intent.
Sieben had resided in this city for near
ly thirty years. He retired from active
business life some years ago.
Oscar S. Meysel, E. R. Randlett and
William. Hammond St., all particular
friends of the dead man, believe that he
had become insane through worrying over
the ghastly evidence in the Brandes trial
and 'was in that state when he fired the
fatal shot into his brain.
been quite 111 for six months, but his mnntal
trouble, 1 think, dates back to the time ot the
Brandes trial. We have frequently noticed
that he acted strangely, but never had any

Refuses to Produce Books.
OAKLAND, Feb. 11.â G. B. Daniels of
the Enquirer < Pubiishing Company, who,
with Dr. George C." Pardee," Henry C. Dal
ton and others, is' being sued by D. Ed
ward Collins > for an accounting, . was
purged of contempt . of . court to-day , for
refusing>to answer the questions put to
him by Attorney Henry Ach before Court
Commissioner^ George C. Babcock. The
whole preceding depended upon the ques
tion of Jurisdiction. Judge Ogden decided
that' his court does' not have jurisdiction
in cases of depositions. "If there was any
contempt," he said, "it is the Court Com
missioner who is offended, and it devolves
upon him to punish the offenderâ if he
can." -; *'
-The fate of Daniels wa3 decided by the
ruling of the Supreme Court in' tha case of
Levinsky, a defendant in Contra Costa
County, who was haled before the Supe
rior Court for refusing to answer the
questions of a notary public. There the
court held that the Superior Court had
no juri8Giction to punish, and that the de
fendant was in contempt only to tne no
tary, who naa no power to punish disobe
dience of his rulings. Judge Ogden held
that the Levinsky case and tne Daniels
case, was Identical, with the exception
that in one case the referee was a notary
and in the other a Court Commissioner. -
This ' decision means that Uaniela and
the others" do mqi have to answer any
more question in deposition taxing unless
they wish, - ' â¢

MRS. PETERMAN
ADJUDGED SANE
Court Settles Fight Over
Guardianship of Big
Estate.
Erstwhile Incompetent Now
to Resume Control of
Salt Lands.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
HIS Broadway, Feb. 11.
The legal entanglements over the guard
ianship of the person and property of
Mrs. Mary Peterman, whose estate in
cludes large tracts of salt-producing
marsh land near Mount Eden, were un
raveled to-day by Judge Ellsworth re
storing, the aged woman to competency.
The proceedings leading to this result
were Instituted by Henry I* Peterman,
who also had his mother declared incom
petent a year ago and himself appointed
as her guardian. A petition was before
the court in the hearing just concluded In
which the other sons and the daughters
of Mrs. Peterman re<juested the removal
of Henry as guardian and the appoint
ment of some one else in # hls stead. Oa
the witness stand to-day these protest
ants withdrew the statements made in
the petition and joined in the request to
have their mother restored to compe
tency.
Commenting on the contradictory na
ture of the evidence before him. Judge
Ellsworth said that he thought Mrs. Pe
terman a good deal more competent than
several other witnesses in the case and
that with proper outside advice she ought
to be able to attend to her business.
The property involved was originally
worth about $50,000 to the estate, but is
now heavily lncumbered. The marsh
lands were at one time leased, to the
Federal Salt Company, but it has been
prevented from deriving revenue from
them by suits begun by Henry Peterman
as his mother's guardian, charging that
the lease was obtained by undue influ
ence. These actions are still pending.
Henry Peterman claims that negotia
tions are now pending which will clear
the estate and leave hi3 mother a good
income, amounting to about $1500 a year.
The original lease from the Federal Salt
Company was for $500 a year, and it wa3
this that caused the incompetency pro
ceedings about a year ago.
LANDERS STEVENS INFLICTS
SERIOUS WOUND ON" HIMSELF
OAKLAND, Feb. 1Lâ Landers Stevens,
the manager of the Dewey Theater, is
confined to his bed with a very serious
wound in his right leg, accidentally in
flicted by himself after the performance
of the 'Blue and the Gray" last evening.
Mr. Stevens was trying to drag from its
sheath a blade of a sword cane. "When
the blade, was finally drawn it stuck into
his right leg, and the actor-manager is
now in bed, trying to ward off a case of
biood-polsoning. The blade wa3 rusty,
and it is feared that it may have infected
the wound.
"The worst of it all is that it was done
after the performance, and even my own
company thought that I was fooling,"
said -Mr. Stevens. "I have a sword com
bat in the play, but there was no such
luck as to have it happen during that
fight."
Mr. Stevens wfll be compelled to remain
in bed for some time, and it has been
found necessary to change the bill for
next week and put on "Romeo and
Juliet", because of this accident.

FOB PACIFIC UNION CLUB
.All the officials of thÂ« Southern 1 Pacific
Company who also hold positions with
the Market-street- Railway Company have
been notified by President E.'H. Hatri
man that they must sever their connec
tions with the latter^ corporation. As a
result they have all handed in their resig
nations â¢ to the Market-street road with
the exception of R. P. Schwerin, the man
ager of the purchasing department for
both roads. President Harriman, allowed
him to hold both positions as a matter
of policy. . Â¦ Y â¢;-. ,
The executive committee of the board of
directors of the Market-street Railway
will meet to-morrow, and it is definitely
known that the first business brought up
will be the acceptance of the resignations.
Those who are affected by the new order
of President Harriman are W. F. Herrin,
chief counsel; Dr. M. Gardner, who
holds the position of chief
surgeon; David R. Sessions, claims
attorney; Captain N. T. Smith, treasurer,
and Major J. M. Hanford, paymaster.
These officials have all sent in their resig
nations, deciding that when it , comes to
real business the Southern Pacific, with
Harriman at the, wheel, is the best of the
two corporations to stay with.
Scb.werin.'s Aid Desired.
The diplomatic hand of Harriman is
shown by his graciously consenting to al
low R. P. Schwerin to still act as pur
chasing agent for both roads. His reason
for allowing Schwerin to remain is solely
for business motives. President Harri
man, after due consideration of the mat
ter, decided that should. Schwerin resign
from the Market-street Railway his suc
cessor would possibly not be as friendly
with the Southern Pacific. The street-car
corporation receives : annually vast
amounts of supplies from the East over
tho Harriman roads, and the big railroad
magnate, with a clear . eye for freight
business, decided that with Schwerin still
purchasing agent there would be no fear
of any of this freight business going
over the Santa Fe lines.:
Benefits Expected to j Result.
A prominent railroad man said that
the resignation of the officials connected
with both corporations is simply the out
come of the new ownership of the South
ern Pacific. He explained that in the old
Huntlngton days' the two companies wer<j
owned by the same Interests, but to-day
the stock Is held by entirely different cap
italists, who have nothing in common. . .
"I think it is a very wise move on the
part of President Harriman," he said.' "it
is a bad policy for two companies- that
are so extensive as the Southerir Pacific
and the Market-street Railway to transact
their business together under the same
roof and attended to by the same officials.
These resignations are no surprise to me
and I think the change will be of great
benefit to both corporations. >

for Important Business â¢

whereupon- the presentee placed his own

jority of the presentees, therefore, had to
be contented with a simple; bow. Crowds
of peoole witnessed the arrival and de

The crisis of the disease is still to come
and is looked for within the next twenty
four or thirty-six hours. The President
and his wife believe their son. will meet
this crisis in good shape, and, passing it
successfully, will recover. In fact, the
slight favorable turn in the disease to
day has greatly relieved the strain on
both of them and the day closed much
more cheerfully than yesterday. Still the
reports issued through Mr. Cortelyou, sec
retary to the President, ran between hope
and fear. An interesting event of the day
was the receipt of a telegram from,J_m
bassador Pauncefote at Washingtontcon.
veying King Edward's expression of sym
pathy for the President and hope for. the
speedy recovery 'of his son. | - ' Â« *
BERLIN, Feb. 12.â At the farewell din
ner given last night by Emperor William
to Prince Henry, Andrew D. White, the
American Embassador, occupied a seat on
the right of his Majesty, while Count von
Bulow, the Imperial Chancellor, sat on
Emperor William's left. â¢ The other mem
bers of the American embassy present
were John B. Jackson, secretary; Com
mander William H. Beehler, naval at
tache, and Major John B. Kerr, the mil
itary attache. Admiral von Tlrpitz, sec
retary of the German Admiralty; Vice
Admiral von Seckendorff, Herr von Eisen
decher, the Prussian representative at
Carlsruhe, Baden, members of Prince
Henry's American party, were also pres
ent. "During the dinner Emperor William
was chatty, jolly and interesting, and
English was the only language used. Em
bassador White showed the Emoeror a
cable received from Secretary of State
Hay saying that the son of President
Roosevelt was better. After dinner the
party spent p.n hour In the library, smok
ing. Emperor William, Mr. White and all
the others then accompanied Prince
Henry to the railroad station, the train
leaving for Kiel at a few minutes after
midnight.

The agreement is signed by Lord Lans
downe and Baron Hayashi, the. Japanese
Minister to Great Britain. These Minis
ters so well kept the secret that the paper
Issued after Parliament had adjourned
for the night, announcing the first impor
tant alliance between a Western and an
Asian race, comes as a startling surprise
to the public, and although the idea of an
alliance with Japan is likely to meet with
general approval, the outcome of this sen
sational departure will be anticipated with
no little anxiety. It is regarded as a di
rect move against Russia and to explain
the abandonment of the colony of Wei Hai
Wei." Â¦ .. "- ' Â¦"Â¦Â¦ . - >Â¦ Â¦ Â¦ ,-; -â¢ Â¦*--,
"We each says Lord Lans
downe, "that the integrity and indepen
dence of the Chinese Empire should be
preserved, and that there should be no
disturbance of the territorial statu quo
either In China or the adjoining regions "
The discovery that their Eastern poli
cies were identical resulted in each party
to the agreement expressing its desire that
their common policy find expression in an
international contract of binding validity
Lord Lansdowne further says that the
British Government was largely Influenced
in entering upon this important contract
by the conviction that it contains no pro
vision which can be regarded as aggres
sive or self-seeking. He says it is con
ceded merely as a measure of precaution
and that it in no way threatens the pres- 1
ent position of legitimate interests of other
powers.. His Majesty's Government trusts
that the agreement will be of mutual ad
vantage to the two countries and make
for the preservation of peace, and, should
peace unfortunately be broken, it will
have the effect of restricting ; the area of
hostilities. Â¦ Â¦ Â¦ . Â¦ â¢Â¦ Â¦ . .Â¦-.-, .. .
In explanation the paper says the agree
ment may be regarded as an outcome of
the events of the past two years. Through
out the Boxer troubles Great Britain and
Japan had been in close and uninterrupted
communication and actuated by similar
views. .

SANTA CRUZ. Feb. 1Lâ Louis . Casselli
came to Santa Cruz last September from
Hanford, where he had been in the hotel
business. He decided to purchase a hotel
in Watsonville, and on September 19 came
from that place after inspecting the ho
tel, hitched his horse in front of Gari
baldi Villa and engaged a room. A half
hour later he disappeared and has never
been seen since. He left a waistcoat
containing a watch, a chain and a ring
in the room. Although relatives and offi
cers have made a thoroug-h search not
the least trace of him can be fourid.
Casselll is 36 years old. His relatives
have offered a reward for information
that may reveal his whereabouts.
Choen Koen, his assailant, was charged
at the City Prison with murder. He said
that he had been following Ching Ging
for two vears. He alleges that the dead
man stole his* wife. He said that yester
day was the first opportunity he had to
get revenge.
Hardly had the patrol wagon taken
away the first man when Sergeant Conboy
saw a highbinder fire two shots into an
other Chinese. The victim dropped in his
tracks and Sergeant Conboy gave chase
to his murderer. The murderer, ran into
a lodging-house at S44 Washington street
and Conboy fired several shots into the
floor, hoping to frighten him. The fugi
tive paid no heed to the shots, however,
and endeavored to escape by way of an.
exit on the Stockton street side, but the
Chlng Gins was the first victim. He
â¢was standing at the corner of Washington
street and Church alley shortly after i
o'clock when Choen Koen stepped up be
hind him, pulled a revolver from under
his blouse and fired two shots into his
body. Police Officer Tillmann was near
by and quickljv had the assassin under
arrest. Ching- Ging, who was- mortally
wounded, was hurried to the Central
Emergency Hospital, where he died about
fifteen minutes after his arrival. One
bullet entered his back, near the spinal
column, passing- completely through his
body. Tfce other bullet passed through
his right arm and was found by Dr.
James Murphy in the silk blouse.
PISTOLS rang out in Chinatown
yesterday afternoon and two Chi
nese were almost instantly Wiled
Their murderers were caught and
are now in the City Prison.

Grudge.

"Many of my friends have quit coffee
and are using Postum and I do not know
of a single one who has not been bene-
fited. Some of these did not like Postum
at first, but in every case I found the
cause to have been not having boiled it
long enough. The directions are" easy
enough to follow, and when followed a
delicious beverage is the result, but some
slovenly cooks put It on to boil for five or
ten minutes, but that does not make 'good
Postum. After the real bubbling or boil-
ing has begun it should be allowed to
boll at least fifteen minutes." Name given
by Postura Co., Battle Creek, Mich.
"I have now been drinking Postum for
over a year and my health has steadily
Improved; the nervousness and dyspepsia
have entirely disappeared. I have gained
twenty-five pounds in weight and feel/ at
least, ten years younger than I did a year
ago. ..., ...â
"A friend insisted that I quit coffee and
try Postum, and, although I did not be-
lieve it was the cause of my ill health, I
consented to try the new coffee. I
bought a package and wife prepared it
strictly according to directions and I was
wonderfully pleased to find It so delicious
and refreshing; the quitting of coffee was
a pleasure rather than a task.
A man In West Plains, Mo., writes: "As
the coffee habit grew on me my health
declined in the same ratio. I was subject
to nervousness, constipation, dyspepsia
and a general weak feeling which made
me unfit for business, and finally I be-
came so nervous and thoroughly broken
down that I could not; attend to business
and turned everything^over to my assist-
ant and went to the mountains In Colo-
rado, hoping to find health in a change of
climate, but after six months' sojourn I
came back in worse condition than I left.
But once In a while a person wakes up
and throws off coffee entirely, then the
truth begins to appear, and it is an easy
job to make this change if Postum Pood
Coffee is taken in place of coffee.
They will charge the coated tongue and
j bilious condition to something they have
eaten, likewise the wretched condition of
the bowels and the palpitation of the
heart. The sallow complexion is generally
attributed to a "naturally Wlious" tem-
perament.
It is curious to watch a coffee toper and
observe how he or she will resist all sug-
gestions as to coffee being the cause of
their various ailments.

Steamer Movements.
A red and black horizontally striped buoy
will be established to mark a. lump near the
entrance to the South Channel, and about 8%
miles SSW. % W. from Cape Disappointment
lighthouse.
The bearings and distances given above are'
approximate only and a corrected notice will
be issued when the buoys are placed.
By order of the Lighthouse Board.
W. P. Day, Commander, U. S. N.,
Lighthouse Inspector.
A bell buoy will be placed in position about
four miles SSW. Y4. W. from Cape Disappoint
ment lighthouse to mark the entrance to the
South Channel, and the Inner Barj Buoy will
be moved to tbe westward and placed on same
bearing, distant about 2% miles from the light.
The latter buoy will then serve as a turning
buoy for vessels entering by either the North
or South Channel. Â¦ . .
SOUTH CHANNEL.
The Whistler, Outer Buoy (M. S.), and Bar
Buoy will be moved to the northward and all
placed In a range with Cape Disappointment
lighthouse. The range line on entering will be
ENE. % E. After passing the Bar Buoy, the
course to be steered to the Inner Buoy (new po
sition) will be SE. Vt, S.

The steamship Thyra, which arrived
here on Monday from the Orient, has been
chartered from the. California-Oriental
line by the Oregon Railroad and Naviga
tion Company to take the place of the
steamship Knfght Companion, which was
recently wrecked in the China Sea.
"Will Replace the Knight Companion.
The steam schooners Del Norte and
Crescent City are now and have been for
five days off Crescent City, kept outside
by stormy weather. The Crescent City
managed to get in long enough to land
her passengers. The elements drove her
to v sea again, however, and with the Del
Norte she is playing Flying Dutchman up
and down the coast. <Â»
TJnable to Enter Crescent City.
Steamship Queen Has Hough Passage
The steamship Queen arrived yesterday
from the Sound after a very disagreeable
passage. She encountered head winds and
heavy seas, and rain, 'more or less heavy,
prevailed throughout the trip. Among the
passengers was Captain A. C. Showman,
who was last here as one of the officers
of the steamship Moana. He left that
vessel In Victoria and may remain per
manently in California.
Laura Pike an. Old Timer.
The schooner Laura Pike, which was
abandoned off Cape Mendocino on the 7th
inst. by captain and crew, is an old-timer.
Bhe was built twenty-seven years ago in
Eureka- Unless she has capsized she
Is now having the spree of her life. When
abandoned she had jib and mainsail set,

Second Officer Fred Franks of the steam
ship Doric was held up early yesterday
morning at Second and Brannan streets
and robbed of $25. He was on his way
aboard ship when two men stepped out
of a shadow and one of them stuck a re
volver under his nose. The, other went
through his pockets. Before the pocket
searcher had completed his task the sound
of approaching footsteps 'alarmed the
footpads, who vanished into the. night.
The feet that sounded the alarm belonged
to Third Officer J. Dennison, also of the
Doric. Franks and Dennison tried to lo
cate the men of the road, Intending to
hold them up, but the disappearance had
been managed perfectly, and after an
hour's tramp the sailormen gave up the
chase.
I>orio Officer Meets Footpads.

gers and the usual big cargo of
island freight. Her time from port to
port was five days twenty hours, and
this through seme of the dirtiest weather
that has prevailed on the Pa'cific for a
long time. The Alameda got into port
just ir. time, for an hour after she en
tered the harbor the bar began to break
to the extent of effectually locking the
Golden Gate for the night. It was pretty
rough when the pilot boarded the steam
ship, but just the same a record for pilot
catching was established which will be
hard to beat. When the dinsey contain
ing Pilot Kortz was sighted the Ala
meda's course Teas directed toward the
little craft. At 3:21 p. m. Captain Herri -
inan gave the signal to stop. At 3:22 p.
m. the signal "full speed ahead" was
given. In the interval the dingey had
come alongside, Kortz had grabbed the
Jacob's ladder, climbed up the side and
v.-as aboard the steamship. This would
be a short stop with the water like a
millpond. When yesterday's record was
made there was a big sea en, the liner
was pitching heavily and the dingey was
tossing up and down like a feather In a
March gale. Two stowaways on board
the Alameda caused the quarantine au
thorities to detain the vessel for about
an hour. The plague in Honolulu is said
to have disappeared altogether, but the
authorities still insist upon the steerage
passengers undergoing an inspection.
This formality the stowaways for obvious
reasons tailed to observe.

President Smith, when seen to-dav,
made the following statement in regard to
this section of the report:
Among the specified statements by
church leaders on the questions dwelt on
in the report is the following: , Â¦
Â¦ "Joseph F. Smith, formerly chief coun
sellor to President Snow and now presi
dent of the church, said in 1896, while
dedicating a meeting house in Payson:
'Take care of your polygamous wives. We
don't care for Uncle Sam now.' "
Third â That the Mormon authorities continue
to publicly teach and insist upon the rightful
ness of polygamy Is abundantly proven by
public utterances of high. officials.
First â Polygamous cohabitation aÂ« evidenced
by the birth of children or the acknowledgment
of parties concerned or concurrent testimony of
neighbors, and sometimes by all of these,. Is
proven to continue in multiplied Instances in
Salt' Lake City, Logan, Brig ham City, Provo,
Payson, Smithfleld, American Fork, Prlce t
Sandy, Tooele and elsewhere In Utah, and also
In Paiisiyand Montpeller, In Idaho.
Second â That plural marriages have been
contracted since the church manifesto was Is
sued Is shown by the returns giving the names
of parties In several of the above mentioned
places, some of- the new plural wives not hav
ing come to a marriageable age, as early as
181K). the date of that document.
The returns coming from parties whose re
liability can in every case be. vouched for by
members of this body show the following con
ditions prevailing at the present time:
This report is to be used as a petition
for the passage of a constitutional amend
ment prohibiting polygamy, and will be
sent to the Judiciary committees of Con
gress. The report, which is quite lengthy,
sets forth that positive evidence can be
produced that polygamy Is both practiced
and taught by the Mormons at the pres
ent time in open defiance of the laws of
the State.' The document contains numer
ous quoted statements "alleged to have
been made by Mormon leaders in which
the practice and teaching of polygamy are
advocated and urged, regardlefs of man
made laws to the contrary.
The document is the' outgrowth of nu
merous meetings of the alliance, resulting
several months ago in the appointment
of a. committee to investigate thoroughly
the reports of the continued practice of
polygamy, such evidence to be used in the
campaign for a constitutional amendment
against polygamy. The ministers
that the evidence is in such. shape that it
could be produced Â¦ as evidence in court
if it became necessary to do so. Besides
being sent to the Judiciary committees of
Congress, the document, it is said, is to
be extensively circulated in the East to
further the. campaign for the anti-polyg
amy amendment to the constitution.
The report, after quoting a number of
Mormon. leaders In favor of the practice
of. polygamy, dwells at some length. upon
the colonization schemes of the church,
which, according to the committee's re
port, have a twofold purpose, viz.: To
enable the Mormons to practice polygamy
"where they will be free from the,scru
tiny of- their Gentile neighbors," and to
secure the balance of political power in
the ; sparsely settled States of the "West,
thus blocking the way of any proposed
hostile national legislation. /The report
sums up as follows:
SALT. LAKE CITY, Utah. Feb. 11.â The
Ministerial Alliance of Salt Lake has
unanimously -adopted } the report of the
committee of that organization appointed
to investigate and report upon the alleged
practice and teaching of polygamy by the
Mormons at tho present time.

Beans and Seeds.
BOSTON, Feb. 11. â The wool market. at the
present time is In excellent condition. The
demand is moderate, but some fair sales have,
been made and good prices are received on
transactions. .The announcement was made
by the American Woolen - Company that the
Providence, National,. Riverside, Manton and
Weybosset mills, which have been "shut down
because of labor troubles, would be reopened,
causing general satisfaction. While no boom
is expected a steady trade " is anticipated.
Shipments since January 1 show an increase
of over 8,000,000 pounds over the same period
of 1001 and about 11,000,000 pounds more than
the receipts since the begln'nlng of -the present
year. Territory wools hold firm and the stock
of better wools has become considerably re
duced. Holders of good wools maintain full
market rates and buyers In need of supplies
readily pay these figures: For fine territory
wools the scoured basis. Is 47@49c, with some
choice lots up to 50c. For fine medium wools
about 43@45c; scoured is the range of sales,
the latter" being for good lots. The market for
medium territory wools is quotable at 38@40c,
clean and choice lines up to 42c cleaned.
Oregon wools are steady on the basis of 4i@40o
for fine medium and fine, with valley at 4ti@
48c; staple at 47@50c.
Boston Wool Market.
Quotations for Hay stand the same. The
rains naturally give the market an easier tone,
but they are still confined to the northern and
coast counties "and are therefore not sufficiently
widespread to actually lower prices. Receipts
are light and buyers are purchasing from hand
to mouth. . '
There Is no further change in Feedstuffs.
but the market Is easy, owing to the rain.

NEW YORK STOCK LIST.
NEW YORK. Feb. ll.â To-day's stock mar
ket showed a further expansion in the deal
ings and the great irregularity of the market
continued. The selling side was rather better
represented than yesterday, but many promi
nent stocks took up the advance at new
points. The disposition to take profits was
very natural on the eve of a holiday There
was obviously some misgiving also among
speculative buyers lest the expectations of
coming deals would not be realized. This was
especially the case among local tractions,
where the glamour of the coming announce
ment of the. Metropolitan Street Railway deal
has suffered some decline. The anxiety on the
subject was keener as an announcement is
thought possible over the holiday. Recent
sanguine expectations of benefits to accrue
to the express companies from some sort of
consolidation suffered from eclipse as shown
by the sharp drop of 7% In United' States Ex
press, although the stock finally rallied 4%
American Express, after rising nearly five
points, lost it again. There were however
significant points of strength in the market!
The Steel stocks were all very heavily bought
on encouraging trade prospects, but the
United States Steel stocks met enormous of
ferings at every slight advance. The late
movement in Pennsylvania was regarded as
an alternating movement with that in South
ern Pacific and due to operations by the same
interest. Texas and Pacific was advanced on
the strength of the Increase In the yearly dis
bursement on the company's second incomes.
The cause of the strength In the Iowa Cen
tral stocks and the Minneapolis and St. Louis
stocks was the supposed competition for con
trol between the Illinois Central and Rock
Island. There was very large buying of
Southern Railway and Louisville and Nash
ville and of a number of stocks of compara
tively email capitalization, which was not ex
plained. The buying of the Readings con
tinued very large, but they showed the effect
of profit taking. . The industrials as a rule
outside of the Steel stocks and some of the
Gas stocks, were Inclined to heaviness. Amal
gamated Copper showed a good recovery, but
closed with a small net loss. The closing was
active end irregular.
Business | in bonds continued very large and
the price movement irregular. Total sales,
par value. $4,640,000.
United States new 4s advanced % per cent
on the last call.

Weather Report.
In the central and northern portions of the
State the weather has been considerably warm
er during the week, and cool weather has pre
vailed in the south, with occasional frosts.
Rain has fallen in all sections north of th>
TehachapL The precipitation In the San Joa
<iuin Vclley and the southern coast counties
WÂ£s light, but In the Sacramento Valley and
th* northern coast and bay counties tire rain
fall was heavy. In come places interfering with
farm work. There Is a good supply of enow
In The mountains.
Grain Is in excellent condition in all the cen
tral and northern districts, and prospects con
tinue food for abundant crops. In portions
of the-Â£an Jcaquin Valley and Southern Cali
fornia more rain will be needed very soon to
make even a fair crop of grain. The warm
weather has caused a rapid growth of grain
and green feed. Pasturage Is abundant in
most places, and stock are doing well. Plow
ing and seeding are progressing In portions of
the Fan Joaquin Valley. Southern California
and the lower coast counties.
The orange crop is reported below expecta
tions In Southern California, but the truit is
mostly cf excellent flavor. Work In orchards
and vineyards Is progressing, and trees ar.i
vines are In first-class condition. Almonds are
in bloom in some sections. Apricot buds are
reported light in portions of the Sacramento
Valley. Deciduous fruit prospects are consid
ered excellent in nearly all places.
SACRAMENTO VALLEY.â Warm weather
has prevailed during the week, and rain has
fallen In all sections, greatly improving the
condition of grain and green feed. The sea
sonal rainfall la below the average, but the
Â¦oil Is in excellent condition. There Is a good
supply of snow In the mountains. Grain has
made rapid growth during the warm days, and
Is looking etrong and healthy: prospects are
good for a heavy yield of 'wheat and barley.
Pasturage Is plentiful, and stock are In good
condition. Orchards and vineyards are in first
class condition, and give Indications of good
crops. Almond trees are blooming in portions
of Yolo County. Apricot budÂ» are reported
light In some places.
COAST AND BAY SECTIONS.â Conditions
have been generally favorable for crops dur
ing the week, but heavy rains In the northern
counties have eomewhat retarded work In fields,
orchards and vineyards. The warm weather
has caused a rapid growth of grain and green
feed in all sections. The rainfall in the south
ern districts has been lighter than in the north,
but sufficient to greatly Improve crop prospects
and enable farmers to resume plowing and
eeedlng. Grain Is In excellent condition, and
large crops are probable. Pasturage is abund
ant, and stock are doing well. Orchards and
vineyards are in excellent condition.
SAN JOAQUIN VALLEY. â 'Warm weather
has prevailed during the week, and light rain
has fallen In the central and northern por
tions of the valley. The seasonal rainfall in
all sections la far below the average, and 1Â»
the southern district* crop prospects are not
good. Grain Is in fair condition In the north
ern counties, and has made rapid growth dar
ing th* warm weather. Green feed is very
scarce In some sections. Plowing, seeding and
pruning are progressing. Orchards and vine
yards are in excellent condition, and prospects
for an average yield of deciduous fruits and
grapes are very good. Irrigation water is
plentiful.
SOUTHERN CALIFORNIA.â The weather
has continued cool during the week, especially
along the coast, and frosts have occurred in
some eectlons. No rain nas fallen since last
Monday, but the rainfall of the preceding week
bag proved of great benefit to all crops. Grain
â¢own in December Is In excellent condition,
and green feed is becoming plentiful. More
rain will be needed eoon to insure good crops.
Water in the wells Is falling rapidly in por
tions of Orange County, and snow is disappear
ing from the mountains. Orchards and vine
yards are in good condition, and â¢work is pro
gr re seing.
LOS ANGELES SUMMARY.â Unsettled
â¢weather during most of the week, but no rain.

ft vegetation. Top soil Is crusting and rain
I* badly needed. Oranges are in good demand;
the crop Is lighter than expected In places.

CHICAGO, Feb. 11. â The trade in wheat was
still much in the same rut as for the past week.
Information was divided rather evenly between
the opposing forces and It was largely the even
ing up over to-morrow that brought out the
scanty strength that held to the cereal. North
western receipts were light, but advices had it
that there was plenty of . wheat there that
would soon > come out. Reports from Oklahoma
and Kansas were unfavorable for the winter
crop and were regarded merely as the forerun
ners of a long series of complaints about crop
disasters which will accompany the opening of
spring. Some strength developed early, in corn
and oats, and when Bradstreet's reported a de
crease in the world's available supply of 3,423,
000 bushels compared with a large increase a
year ago wheat was helped upward. Trade on
the whole was email, but that also probably
was due to to-morrow's holiday. Liverpool
cables were steady to start with, but eased off.
May opened a shade to ' %c up at 77%@77%c,
sold up to 78c and eased back to the opening
prices. May closed firm at its top figure, %@
%c higher, at 78%c.
Corn was lower at the opening on lower ca
bles and some forced selling. Covering and the
better tone in wheat . and Â¦ oats later caused a
rally. The close was firm, May%c up at 61%

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Receipts of Butter were more liberal, and
while the market was momentarily firm the
firmness Is not expected to last in view of the
warm, moist weather, which is very favorable
to a large increase in the production. ' There
are some sales of fancy creamery at 29c, but
they form a small proportion of the aggregate.
The receipts of Eggs, as will be seen below,
were heavy, and nothing but the light spot
stocks prevented a break In prices. There were
more ' on the market, however, - and if the ar
rivals continue large- for a day or so a decline
will" probably result. Some dealers are report
ing sales of extra fine ranch at 22c,'~but this
figure Is rather extreme for a positive quota
tion.
Cheese continues steady and without notice
able change. . . ' - . '

to for several years, was placed on trial
before a jury yesterday- in the United
States District Court on a charge of
forgery. Wilgus is charged in the indict
ment with having forged the name of
Captain R. B. Wilkinson of Company A,
One Hundredth" Pennsylvania Regiment
of Volunteers, â¢ to an affidav.i. reciting that
William- Matthews; alias James Brown,
a colored man, had been a member of
Company B, First Regiment of Volun
teers, during the Civil War. Matthews
was acauitted on' Monday, there having
been a doubt In the minds "o( the Jurors
as to whether he might not have been a
member ; of; the regiment, although his
name did not appear on the roll.
